         <img alt="A black circle outlined in hazy streaks of white emanating from the circle to the edges of the image all on a solid black background" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="431" data-file-width="432" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Solar_eclipse_22_July_2009_taken_by_Lutfar_Rahman_Nirjhar_from_Bangladesh.jpg/200px-Solar_eclipse_22_July_2009_taken_by_Lutfar_Rahman_Nirjhar_from_Bangladesh.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Solar_eclipse_22_July_2009_taken_by_Lutfar_Rahman_Nirjhar_from_Bangladesh.jpg/300px-Solar_eclipse_22_July_2009_taken_by_Lutfar_Rahman_Nirjhar_from_Bangladesh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Solar_eclipse_22_July_2009_taken_by_Lutfar_Rahman_Nirjhar_from_Bangladesh.jpg/400px-Solar_eclipse_22_July_2009_taken_by_Lutfar_Rahman_Nirjhar_from_Bangladesh.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solar_eclipse_22_July_2009_taken_by_Lutfar_Rahman_Nirjhar_from_Bangladesh.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Total
         <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_July_22,_2009" title="Solar eclipse of July 22, 2009">
          solar eclipse of July 22, 2009
         </a>
         , from
         <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">
          Bangladesh
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       During the 21st century, there will be 224
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse" title="Solar eclipse">
        solar eclipses
       </a>
       of which 77 will be partial, 72 will be annular, 68 will be total and 7 will be hybrids between total and annular eclipses. Of these, two annular and one total eclipse will be non-central,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_catalog_1-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nasa_catalog-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in the sense that the very center (axis) of the moon's shadow will miss the earth (for more information see
       <a href="/wiki/Gamma_(eclipse)" title="Gamma (eclipse)">
        gamma
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glossary_2-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-glossary-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the 21st century the greatest number of eclipses in one year is four, in 2011, 2029, 2047, 2065, 2076, and 2094. The predictions given here are by
       <a href="/wiki/Fred_Espenak" title="Fred Espenak">
        Fred Espenak
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
        NASA
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/Goddard_Space_Flight_Center" title="Goddard Space Flight Center">
        Goddard Space Flight Center
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_catalog_1-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-nasa_catalog-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       At this point, the longest duration in which the moon completely covered the sun, known as
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse#Totality" title="Solar eclipse">
        totality
       </a>
       , was during the
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_July_22,_2009" title="Solar eclipse of July 22, 2009">
        solar eclipse of July 22, 2009
       </a>
       . This total solar eclipse had a maximum duration of 6 minutes and 39 seconds. The longest possible duration of a total solar eclipse is 7 minutes and 32 seconds. The longest annular solar eclipse of the 21st century took place on
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_January_15,_2010" title="Solar eclipse of January 15, 2010">
        January 15, 2010
       </a>
       , with a duration of 11 minutes and 8 seconds. The maximum possible duration is 12 minutes and 29 seconds. The eclipse of
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_May_20,_2050" title="Solar eclipse of May 20, 2050">
        May 20, 2050
       </a>
       , will be the second hybrid eclipse in the span of less than one year, the first one being on
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_November_25,_2049" title="Solar eclipse of November 25, 2049">
        November 25, 2049
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference">
        [
        <a href="#cite_note-listref-a">
         a
        </a>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The next solar eclipse (Partial) will occur on
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_July_13,_2018" title="Solar eclipse of July 13, 2018">
        July 13, 2018
       </a>
       ; the last solar eclipse (Partial) occurred on
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_February_15,_2018" title="Solar eclipse of February 15, 2018">
        February 15, 2018
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The table contains the date and time of the greatest eclipse (in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dynamical_time" title="Dynamical time">
        dynamical time
       </a>
       , which in this case is the time when the axis of the Moon's shadow cone passes closest to the centre of Earth; this is in
       <a href="/wiki/Universal_Time" title="Universal Time">
        Universal Time
       </a>
       ). The number of the
       <a href="/wiki/Saros_(astronomy)" title="Saros (astronomy)">
        saros series
       </a>
       that the eclipse belongs to is given, followed by the
       <a href="/wiki/Magnitude_of_eclipse" title="Magnitude of eclipse">
        magnitude of the eclipse
       </a>
       (the fraction of the Sun's diameter obscured by the Moon) and the type of the eclipse (either total, annular, partial or hybrid). For total and annular eclipses, the duration of the eclipse is given, as well as the location of the greatest eclipse (the point of maximum eclipse) and the path width of the total or annular eclipse. The geographical areas from which the eclipse can be seen are listed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_3-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nasa-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
